Although the number of cases of influenza can vary widely between years, approximately 36,000 deaths and more than 200,000 hospitalizations are directly associated with influenza a year in the United States.[\[188\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-188)[\[189\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-189) One method of calculating influenza mortality produced an estimate of 41,400 average deaths per year in the United States between 1979 and 2001.[\[190\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-190) Different methods in 2010 by the [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention "Centers for Disease Control and Prevention") (CDC) reported a range from a low of about 3,300 deaths to a high of 49,000 per year.[\[191\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-191)

### Outbreaks

As influenza is caused by a variety of species and strains of [viruses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus "Virus"), in any given year some strains can die out while others create [epidemics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epidemic "Epidemic"), while yet another strain can cause a [pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pandemic "Pandemic"). Typically, in a year's normal two [flu seasons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_season "Flu season") (one per hemisphere), there are between three and five million cases of severe illness,[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1)[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-WHO2017-4)[\[192\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Loz2012-192) which by some definitions is a yearly influenza epidemic.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1)

Roughly three times per century, a pandemic occurs, which infects a large proportion of the world's population and can kill tens of millions of people (see [pandemics section](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#Pandemics "Influenza")). In 2006, a study estimated that if a strain with similar [virulence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virulence "Virulence") to the [1918 influenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic "1918 flu pandemic") had emerged that year, it could have killed between 50 and 80 million people.[\[193\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-193)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/220px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_geneticshift.svg)

Antigenic shift, or reassortment, can result in novel and highly pathogenic strains of human influenza

New influenza viruses are constantly [evolving](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution") by [mutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation "Mutation") or by [reassortment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reassortment "Reassortment").[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-hay-51) Mutations can cause small changes in the [hemagglutinin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin "Hemagglutinin") and [neuraminidase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase "Neuraminidase") [antigens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen "Antigen") on the surface of the virus. This is called [antigenic drift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_drift "Antigenic drift"), which slowly creates an increasing variety of strains until one evolves that can infect people who are immune to the pre-existing strains. This new variant then replaces the older strains as it rapidly sweeps through the human population, often causing an epidemic.[\[194\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-194) However, since the strains produced by drift will still be reasonably similar to the older strains, some people will still be immune to them. In contrast, when influenza viruses reassort, they acquire completely new antigensâ€”for example by reassortment between avian strains and human strains; this is called [antigenic shift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_shift "Antigenic shift"). If a human influenza virus is produced that has entirely new antigens, everybody will be susceptible, and the novel influenza will spread uncontrollably, causing a pandemic.[\[195\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-195) In contrast to this model of pandemics based on antigenic drift and shift, an alternative approach has been proposed where the periodic pandemics are produced by interactions of a fixed set of viral strains with a human population with a constantly changing set of immunities to different viral strains.[\[196\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-196)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png/220px-Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png)

The generation time for influenza (the time from one infection to the next) is very short (only 2 days). This explains why influenza epidemics start and finish in a short time scale of only a few months.[\[197\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Ferguson2005-197)

From a public health point of view, flu epidemics spread rapidly and are very difficult to control. Most influenza virus strains are not very infectious and each infected individual will only go on to infect one or two other individuals (the basic reproduction number for influenza is generally around 1.4). However, the generation time for influenza is extremely short: the time from a person becoming infected to when he infects the next person is only two days. The short generation time means that influenza epidemics generally peak at around 2 months and burn out after 3 months: the decision to intervene in an influenza epidemic, therefore, has to be taken early, and the decision is therefore often made on the back of incomplete data. Another problem is that individuals become infectious before they become symptomatic, which means that putting people in quarantine after they become ill is not an effective public health intervention.[\[197\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Ferguson2005-197) For the average person, viral shedding tends to peak on day two, whereas symptoms peak on day three.[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008-26)

## History

### Etymology

The word _Influenza_ comes from the [Italian language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_language "Italian language") meaning "influence" and refers to the cause of the disease; initially, this ascribed illness to unfavorable [astrological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrology "Astrology") influences. It was introduced into English in the mid-eighteenth century during a pan-European epidemic.[\[198\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-198) Archaic terms for influenza include _epidemic catarrh_, _la grippe_ (from the French, first used by Molyneaux in 1694; also used in German),[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199) _sweating sickness_, and _Spanish fever_ (particularly for the [1918 flu pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic "1918 flu pandemic") strain).[\[200\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-200)

### Pandemics

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/220px-W_curve.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:W_curve.png)

The difference between the influenza mortality age distributions of the 1918 epidemic and normal epidemics. Deaths per 100,000 persons in each age group, United States, for the interpandemic years 1911â€“1917 (dashed line) and the pandemic year 1918 (solid line).[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/220px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg)

Thermal imaging camera and screen, photographed in an airport terminal in [Greece](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greece "Greece") during the 2009 flu pandemic. Thermal imaging can detect elevated body temperature, one of the signs of swine flu.

An overall lack of data up until 1500 precludes meaningful search for the influenza outbreaks in the more distant past.[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-flu-202) Possibly the first influenza pandemic occurred around 6000 BC in China.[\[202\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-flu-202) The symptoms of human influenza were clearly described by [Hippocrates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippocrates "Hippocrates") roughly 2,400 years ago.[\[203\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-203)[\[204\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-204) Although the virus seems to have caused epidemics throughout human history, historical data on influenza are difficult to interpret, because the symptoms can be similar to those of other respiratory diseases.[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199)[\[205\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-205) The disease may have spread from Europe to the Americas as early as the [European colonization of the Americas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas "European colonization of the Americas"), since almost the entire indigenous population of the Antilles was killed by an epidemic resembling influenza that broke out in 1493, after the arrival of [Christopher Columbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Columbus "Christopher Columbus").[\[206\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-206)[\[207\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-207)

The first convincing record of an influenza pandemic was a minor pandemic [chronicled in 1510](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1510_influenza_pandemic "1510 influenza pandemic"), which began in East Asia before spreading to North Africa and then Europe. During this pandemic, influenza killed about 1% of its victims.[\[208\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-208)[\[209\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-209) The first pandemic of influenza to be reliably recorded as spreading worldwide was the [1557 influenza pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1557_influenza_pandemic "1557 influenza pandemic"),[\[210\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-:17-210)[\[211\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-:00-211)[\[212\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-:3-212)[\[213\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-:4-213) in which a reoccurring wave likely killed [Queen Mary I of England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_I_of_England "Mary I of England") and the [Archbishop of Canterbury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reginald_Pole "Reginald Pole") within 12 hours of each other.\[_[dead link](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot "Wikipedia:Link rot")_\]\[_[unreliable source?](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources "Wikipedia:Reliable sources")_\][\[214\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-:13-214)[\[215\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-215) One of the most well-chronicled pandemics of influenza in the 16th Century occurred in 1580, beginning in East Asia and spreading to Europe through Africa, Russia, and the Spanish and Ottoman Empires. In [Rome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Rome#Renaissance_Rome "History of Rome"), over 8,000 people were killed. Several Spanish cities saw large scale deaths, among the fatalities the Queen of Spain, [Anna of Austria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anna_of_Austria,_Queen_of_Spain "Anna of Austria, Queen of Spain"). Pandemics continued sporadically throughout the 17th and 18th centuries, with the pandemic of 1830â€“1833 being particularly widespread; it infected approximately a quarter of the people exposed.[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199)

The most lethal outbreak was the [1918 flu pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic "1918 flu pandemic") (Spanish flu) ([type A influenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_A "Influenzavirus A"), [H1N1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1N1 "H1N1") subtype), which lasted into 1920. It is not known exactly how many it killed, but estimates range from 17 million to 100 million people.[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Spreeuwenberg1-18)[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201)[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216)[\[217\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Patterson1-217) This pandemic has been described as "the greatest medical holocaust in history" and may have killed as many people as the [Black Death](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Death "Black Death").[\[199\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199) This huge death toll was caused by an extremely high infection rate of up to 50% and the extreme severity of the symptoms, suspected to be caused by [cytokine storms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytokine_storm "Cytokine storm").[\[217\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Patterson1-217) Symptoms in 1918 were so unusual that initially influenza was misdiagnosed as [dengue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dengue "Dengue"), [cholera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cholera "Cholera"), or typhoid. One observer wrote, "One of the most striking of the complications was hemorrhage from [mucous membranes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mucous_membrane "Mucous membrane"), especially from the nose, stomach, and intestine. Bleeding from the ears and [petechial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petechia "Petechia") hemorrhages in the skin also occurred."[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216) The majority of deaths were from [bacterial pneumonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia "Bacterial pneumonia"), a [secondary infection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_infection "Secondary infection") caused by influenza, but the virus also killed people directly, causing massive [hemorrhages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bleeding "Bleeding") and [edema](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edema "Edema") in the lung.[\[218\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-autogenerated1-218)

The 1918 flu pandemic was truly global, spreading even to the [Arctic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic "Arctic") and remote [Pacific islands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Islands "Pacific Islands"). The unusually severe disease killed between two and twenty percent of those infected, as opposed to the more usual flu epidemic [mortality rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mortality_rate "Mortality rate") of 0.1%.[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201)[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216) Another unusual feature of this pandemic was that it mostly killed young adults, with 99% of pandemic influenza deaths occurring in people under 65, and more than half in young adults 20 to 40 years old.[\[219\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-219) This is unusual since influenza is normally most deadly to the very young (under age 2) and the very old (over age 70). The total mortality of the 1918â€“1919 pandemic is not known, but it is estimated that 2.5% to 5% of the world's population was killed. As many as 25 million may have been killed in the first 25 weeks; in contrast, [HIV/AIDS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AIDS "AIDS") has killed 25 million in its first 25 years.[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216)

Later flu pandemics were not so devastating. They included the 1957 [Asian flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_Flu "Asian Flu") (type A, [H2N2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H2N2 "H2N2") strain), the 1968 [Hong Kong flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong_Kong_Flu "Hong Kong Flu") (type A, [H3N2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H3N2 "H3N2") strain) and the 1977 [Russian flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1977_Russian_flu "1977 Russian flu") (type A, [H1N1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1n1 "H1n1") strain), but even these smaller outbreaks killed millions of people. In later pandemics [antibiotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic "Antibiotic") were available to control secondary infections and this may have helped reduce mortality compared to the Spanish flu of 1918.[\[201\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201)

**Notes**

1.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_ref-240 "Jump up")** Not pandemic, but included for comparison purposes.

  
It was incorrectly assumed that the cause of influenza was bacterial in origin from 1892 (with _[Haemophilus influenzae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae "Haemophilus influenzae")_ being discovered by and suggested as the origin of influenza by [R. F. J. Pfeiffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Friedrich_Johannes_Pfeiffer "Richard Friedrich Johannes Pfeiffer")).[\[245\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Tbnbrgrtl-246) The first influenza virus to be isolated was from poultry, when in 1901, the agent causing a disease called "fowl plague" was passed through [Chamberland filters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chamberland_filter "Chamberland filter"), which have pores that are too small for [bacteria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria "Bacteria") to pass through.[\[246\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-247) However, the conceptual differences between viruses and bacteria as different entities was not fully understood for some time, complicating preventive measures taken during the 1918 influenza pandemic.[\[245\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Tbnbrgrtl-246) The [etiological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etiology "Etiology") cause of influenza, the virus family Orthomyxoviridae, was first discovered in [pigs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pig "Pig") by [Richard Shope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Shope "Richard Shope") in 1931.[\[247\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-248) This discovery was shortly followed by the isolation of the virus from humans by a group headed by [Patrick Laidlaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patrick_Laidlaw "Patrick Laidlaw") at the [Medical Research Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK) "Medical Research Council (UK)") of [the United Kingdom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom") in 1933.[\[248\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-249) However, it was not until [Wendell Stanley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wendell_Stanley "Wendell Stanley") first crystallized [tobacco mosaic virus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobacco_mosaic_virus "Tobacco mosaic virus") in 1935 that the [non-cellular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology) "Cell (biology)") nature of viruses was appreciated.\[_[medical citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine) "Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)")_\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/350px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_subtypes.svg)

The main types of influenza viruses in humans. Solid squares show the appearance of a new strain, causing recurring influenza pandemics. Broken lines indicate uncertain strain identifications.[\[249\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Palese-250)

The first significant step towards preventing influenza was the development in 1944 of a killed-virus vaccine for influenza by [Thomas Francis, Jr.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Francis,_Jr. "Thomas Francis, Jr.") This built on work by Australian [Frank Macfarlane Burnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Macfarlane_Burnet "Frank Macfarlane Burnet"), who showed that the virus lost virulence when it was cultured in fertilized hen's eggs.[\[250\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Nobel-251) Application of this observation by Francis allowed his group of researchers at the [University of Michigan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Michigan "University of Michigan") to develop the first influenza vaccine, with support from the [U.S. Army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Army "United States Army").[\[251\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-252) The Army was deeply involved in this research due to its experience of influenza in [World War I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I "World War I"), when thousands of troops were killed by the virus in a matter of months.[\[216\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216) In comparison to vaccines, the development of anti-influenza drugs has been slower, with [amantadine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amantadine "Amantadine") being licensed in 1966 and, almost thirty years later, the next class of drugs (the [neuraminidase inhibitors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitor "Neuraminidase inhibitor")) being developed.[\[252\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Lynch-253)

## Society and culture

Influenza produces [direct costs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_cost "Variable cost") due to lost [productivity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Productivity "Productivity") and associated medical treatment, as well as [indirect costs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indirect_costs "Indirect costs") of preventive measures. In the United States, seasonal influenza is estimated to result in a total average annual economic cost of over $11â€¯billion, with direct medical costs estimated to be over $3 billion annually.[\[253\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-254) It has been estimated that a future pandemic could cause hundreds of billions of dollars in direct and indirect costs.[\[254\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Bush-255) However, the economic impacts of past pandemics have not been intensively studied, and some authors have suggested that the [Spanish influenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu "Spanish flu") actually had a positive long-term effect on per-capita income growth, despite a large reduction in the working population and severe short-term [depressive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recession "Recession") effects.[\[255\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-256) Other studies have attempted to predict the costs of a pandemic as serious as the 1918 Spanish flu on the [U.S. economy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States "Economy of the United States"), where 30% of all workers became ill, and 2.5% were killed. A 30% sickness rate and a three-week length of illness would decrease the [gross domestic product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product") by 5%. Additional costs would come from medical treatment of 18 million to 45 million people, and total economic costs would be approximately $700 billion.[\[256\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-257)

Preventive costs are also high. Governments worldwide have spent billions of [U.S. dollars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_dollar "United States dollar") preparing and planning for a potential H5N1 avian influenza pandemic, with costs associated with purchasing drugs and vaccines as well as developing [disaster drills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emergency_management "Emergency management") and strategies for improved [border controls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border_control "Border control").[\[257\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Rosenthal-258) On 1 November 2005, [United States President](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_United_States "President of the United States") [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush "George W. Bush") unveiled the National Strategy to Safeguard Against the Danger of Pandemic Influenza[\[254\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Bush-255) backed by a request to [Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress") for $7.1 billion to begin implementing the plan.[\[258\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-259) Internationally, on 18 January 2006, donor nations pledged US$2 billion to combat bird flu at the two-day International Pledging Conference on Avian and Human Influenza held in China.[\[259\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-260)[\[260\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-261)

In an assessment of the 2009 H1N1 pandemic on selected countries in the Southern Hemisphere, data suggest that all countries experienced some time-limited and/or geographically isolated socioeconomic effects and a temporary decrease in tourism most likely due to fear of 2009 H1N1 disease. It is still too early to determine whether the H1N1 pandemic has had any long-term economic effects.[\[261\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-262)\[_[needs update](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_\]

## Research

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/170px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg)

Research on influenza includes studies on [molecular virology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_virology "Molecular virology"), how the virus produces disease ([pathogenesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogenesis "Pathogenesis")), host [immune responses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_response "Immune response"), [viral genomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genomics "Genomics"), and how the virus spreads ([epidemiology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epidemiology "Epidemiology")). These studies help in developing influenza countermeasures; for example, a better understanding of the body's immune system response helps [vaccine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vaccine "Vaccine") development, and a detailed picture of how influenza invades cells aids the development of antiviral drugs. One important [basic research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research "Research") program is the [Influenza Genome Sequencing Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_Genome_Sequencing_Project "Influenza Genome Sequencing Project"), which was initiated in 2004 to create a library of influenza sequences and help clarify which factors make one strain more lethal than another, which genes most affect [immunogenicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunogenicity "Immunogenicity"), and how the virus [evolves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution") over time.[\[262\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-263)

The sequencing of the influenza genome and [recombinant DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recombinant_DNA "Recombinant DNA") technology may accelerate the generation of new vaccine strains by allowing scientists to substitute new antigens into a previously developed vaccine strain.[\[263\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-264) Growing viruses in [cell culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_culture "Cell culture") also promises higher yields, less cost, better quality and surge capacity.[\[264\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-265) Research on a universal influenza A vaccine, targeted against the external domain of the transmembrane viral [M2 protein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M2_protein "M2 protein") (M2e), is being done at the [University of Ghent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Ghent "University of Ghent") by [Walter Fiers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Fiers "Walter Fiers"), [Xavier Saelens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xavier_Saelens "Xavier Saelens") and their team[\[265\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-266)[\[266\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-267)[\[267\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-268) and has now successfully concluded Phase I clinical trials. There has been some research success towards a "universal flu vaccine" that produces antibodies against proteins on the viral coat which mutate less rapidly, and thus a single shot could potentially provide longer-lasting protection.[\[268\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-269)[\[269\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-270)[\[270\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-271)

A number of [biologics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biologic_medical_product "Biologic medical product"), therapeutic vaccines and immunobiologics are also being investigated for treatment of infection caused by viruses. Therapeutic biologics are designed to activate the immune response to virus or antigens. Typically, biologics do not target [metabolic pathways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway "Metabolic pathway") like anti-viral drugs, but stimulate immune cells such as [lymphocytes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphocytes "Lymphocytes"), [macrophages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macrophages "Macrophages"), and/or [antigen-presenting cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell "Antigen-presenting cell"), in an effort to drive an immune response towards a [cytotoxic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytotoxic "Cytotoxic") effect against the virus. Influenza models, such as murine influenza, are convenient models to test the effects of prophylactic and therapeutic biologics. For example, [lymphocyte T-cell immunomodulator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphocyte_T-cell_immunomodulator "Lymphocyte T-cell immunomodulator") inhibits viral growth in the murine model of influenza.[\[271\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-272)

## Other animals

Influenza infects many animal species, and transfer of viral strains between species can occur. [Birds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird "Bird") are thought to be the main [animal reservoirs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Host_(biology) "Host (biology)") of influenza viruses.[\[272\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-273) Most influenza strains are believed to have originated after humans began their intensive domestication of animals about 10,000 years ago.[\[273\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-274) Sixteen forms of [hemagglutinin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin "Hemagglutinin") and nine forms of [neuraminidase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase "Neuraminidase") have been identified. All known subtypes (HxNy) are found in birds, but many subtypes are [endemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic "Endemic") in humans, dogs, horses, and pigs; populations of camels, ferrets, [cats](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat "Cat"), seals, mink, and whales also show evidence of prior infection or exposure to influenza.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-webster-60) Variants of flu virus are sometimes named according to the species the strain is endemic in or adapted to. The main variants named using this convention are: [bird flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avian_influenza "Avian influenza"), [human flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_flu "Human flu"), [swine flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swine_flu "Swine flu"), [horse flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horse_flu "Horse flu") and [dog flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dog_flu "Dog flu"). ([Cat flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat_flu "Cat flu") generally refers to [feline viral rhinotracheitis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feline_viral_rhinotracheitis "Feline viral rhinotracheitis") or [feline calicivirus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feline_calicivirus "Feline calicivirus") and not infection from an influenza virus.) In pigs, horses and dogs, influenza symptoms are similar to humans, with cough, fever and [loss of appetite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anorexia_(symptom) "Anorexia (symptom)").[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-webster-60) The frequency of animal diseases are not as well-studied as human infection, but an outbreak of influenza in harbor seals caused approximately 500 seal deaths off the [New England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_England "New England") coast in 1979â€“1980.[\[274\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-275) However, outbreaks in pigs are common and do not cause severe mortality.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-webster-60) [Vaccines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_vaccine "Influenza vaccine") have also been developed to protect poultry from [avian influenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avian_influenza "Avian influenza"). These vaccines can be effective against multiple strains and are used either as part of a preventive strategy, or combined with [culling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culling "Culling") in attempts to eradicate outbreaks.[\[275\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-276)

### Bird flu

Flu symptoms in birds are variable and can be unspecific.[\[276\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-277) The symptoms following infection with low-pathogenicity avian influenza may be as mild as ruffled feathers, a small reduction in egg production, or [weight loss](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weight_loss "Weight loss") combined with minor [respiratory disease](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Respiratory_disease "Respiratory disease").[\[277\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-278) Since these mild symptoms can make diagnosis in the field difficult, tracking the spread of avian influenza requires laboratory testing of samples from infected birds. Some strains such as Asian [H9N2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H9N2 "H9N2") are highly virulent to poultry and may cause more extreme symptoms and significant mortality.[\[278\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-279) In its most highly pathogenic form, influenza in [chickens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicken "Chicken") and [turkeys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkey_(bird) "Turkey (bird)") produces a sudden appearance of severe symptoms and almost 100% mortality within two days.[\[279\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-280) As the virus spreads rapidly in the crowded conditions seen in the [intensive farming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intensive_farming "Intensive farming") of chickens and turkeys, these outbreaks can cause large economic losses to poultry farmers.\[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_\]

An avian-adapted, highly pathogenic strain of H5N1 (called HPAI A(H5N1), for "highly pathogenic avian influenza virus of type A of subtype H5N1") causes [H5N1 flu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_and_infection_of_H5N1 "Transmission and infection of H5N1"), commonly known as "avian influenza" or simply "bird flu", and is [endemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology) "Endemic (epidemiology)") in many bird populations, especially in [Southeast Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia"). This Asian lineage strain of HPAI A(H5N1) is [spreading globally](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_spread_of_H5N1 "Global spread of H5N1"). It is [epizootic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epizootic "Epizootic") (an epidemic in non-humans) and panzootic (a disease affecting animals of many species, especially over a wide area), killing tens of millions of birds and spurring the culling of hundreds of millions of other birds in an attempt to control its spread. Most references in the media to "bird flu" and most references to H5N1 are about this specific strain.[\[280\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-281)[\[281\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-282)

HPAI A(H5N1) is an avian disease and there is no evidence suggesting efficient human-to-human transmission of HPAI A(H5N1). In almost all cases, those infected have had extensive physical contact with infected birds.[\[282\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-283) H5N1 may mutate or reassort into a strain capable of efficient human-to-human transmission. The exact changes that are required for this to happen are not well understood.[\[283\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-284) Due to the high lethality and [virulence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virulence "Virulence") of H5N1, its [endemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology) "Endemic (epidemiology)") presence, and its large and increasing biological host reservoir, the H5N1 virus was the world's major pandemic threat in the 2006â€“07 flu season, and billions of dollars are being raised and spent researching H5N1 and preparing for a potential influenza pandemic.[\[257\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Rosenthal-258)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/ChineseFluInspectors.JPG/170px-ChineseFluInspectors.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ChineseFluInspectors.JPG)

Chinese inspectors checking airline passengers for fevers, a common symptom of swine flu

In March 2013, the Chinese government reported three cases of H7N9 influenza infections in humans, two of whom had died and the third became critically ill. Although the strain of the virus is not thought to spread efficiently between humans,[\[284\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-285)[\[285\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-286) by mid-April, at least 82 persons had become ill from H7N9, of which 17 had died. These cases include three small family clusters in Shanghai and one cluster between a neighboring girl and boy in Beijing, raising at least the possibility of human-to-human transmission. The WHO points out that one cluster did not have two of the cases lab confirmed and further points out, as a matter of baseline information, that some viruses are able to cause limited human-to-human transmission under conditions of close contact but are not transmissible enough to cause large community outbreaks.[\[286\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-287)[\[287\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-288)[\[288\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-WHO_H7N9-289)

### Swine flu

In pigs [swine influenza](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swine_influenza "Swine influenza") produces fever, lethargy, sneezing, coughing, difficulty breathing and decreased appetite.[\[289\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Kothalawala-290) In some cases the infection can cause abortion. Although mortality is usually low, the virus can produce weight loss and poor growth, causing economic loss to farmers.[\[289\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Kothalawala-290) Infected pigs can lose up to 12 pounds of body weight over a three- to four-week period.[\[289\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Kothalawala-290) Direct transmission of an influenza virus from pigs to humans is occasionally possible (this is called [zoonotic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoonosis "Zoonosis") swine flu). In all, 50 human cases are known to have occurred since the virus was identified in the mid-20th century, which have resulted in six deaths.[\[290\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-Myers-291)

In 2009, a swine-origin [H1N1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1N1 "H1N1") virus strain commonly referred to as "swine flu" caused the [2009 flu pandemic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_flu_pandemic "2009 flu pandemic"), but there is no evidence that it is endemic to pigs (i.e. actually a swine flu) or of transmission from pigs to people; instead, the virus spreads from person to person.[\[291\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-292)[\[292\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-293) This strain is a reassortment of several strains of H1N1 that are usually found separately, in humans, birds, and pigs.[\[293\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza#cite_note-294)
